Puberty Age: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
States Hanafi difference on puberty age and acceptance of claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the minimum age of puberty, identify the evidence each relies on, and explain which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Puberty age: Abu Hanifa says 17 for girls, 18 for boys; Sahibayn say 15 for both – mufta bihi is 15.

This passage from al-Quduri presents an internal Hanafi debate about the age at which a child reaches legal adulthood when no physical signs appear. The difference arises from how the imams interpret the same hadith and apply the principle of ihtiyat (caution). Understanding this ikhtilaf is key to knowing when a minor becomes responsible for obligations like prayer, fasting, and transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine what legal question is being addressed: at what age does a child reach puberty (bulugh) when no physical signs manifest?
The passage first lists physical signs for girls (menstruation, nocturnal emission, pregnancy) and then gives the age-based rule.
2
Distinguish the two positions
Note that Abu Hanifa sets the age at 17 for girls (and by implication 18 for boys), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) set it at 15 for both genders.
The text explicitly gives Abu Hanifa's view first, then 'Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said...'.
3
Trace the evidence
Understand that both sides rely on the hadith of Ibn Umar, but interpret it differently. Abu Hanifa sees permission to fight at 15 as a concession, not proof of puberty; Sahibayn see it as direct evidence.
The principle of ihtiyat (caution) also plays a role in Abu Hanifa's reasoning.
4
Understand the practical consequence
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf: for a 16-year-old girl with no signs, Abu Hanifa considers her a minor, while Sahibayn consider her an adult, affecting her legal capacity for worship and transactions.
This difference impacts rulings on prayer, fasting, marriage, and financial contracts.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Know that the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is the Sahibayn's view of 15 years for both genders, due to ease, predominant practice, and explicit support in Zahir al-Riwayah.
Al-Marghinani and later authorities prefer this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبُلُوغُ الْجَارِيَةِ بِالْخَيْضِ وَالِاحْتِلَامِ، وَالْخَبَلِ فَإِنْ لَمْ يُوجَدْ ذَلِكَ فَحَتَّى يَتِمَّ لَهَا سَبْعَ عَشْرَةَ سَنَةً وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ: إِذَا تَمَّ لِلْغُلَامِ، وَالْجَارِيَةِ خَمْسَ عَشْرَةَ سَنَةً فَقَدْ بَلَغَا وَإِذَا رَاهَقَ الْغُلَامُ، وَالْجَارِيَةُ وَأَشْكَلَ أَمْرُهُمَا فِي الْبُلُوغِ فَقَالَا قَدْ بَلَغْنَا فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُمَا وَأَحْكَامُهُمَا أَحْكَامُ الْبَالِغِينَ
And the puberty of a girl is by menstruation, nocturnal emission, and pregnancy; if that is not found, then until she completes seventeen years. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: When a boy and a girl complete fifteen years, they have reached puberty. And when a boy and a girl approach puberty and their matter regarding puberty is unclear, and they say 'we have reached puberty', then their word is accepted and their rulings are rulings of adults.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage from al-Quduri presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the age of puberty. The author first states Abu Hanifa's position: a girl reaches puberty through physical signs (menstruation, nocturnal emission, or pregnancy). If none appear, she is considered a minor until she completes seventeen lunar years. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) disagree, setting the age of puberty at fifteen years for both boys and girls. They also rule that if a child approaches the age of puberty and claims to have reached it, their word is accepted, and all legal rulings of adulthood apply to them. The commentary (sharh) would discuss the evidence: Abu Hanifa relies on a hadith where Ibn Umar was not allowed to fight at fourteen but was allowed at fifteen, indicating fifteen as the beginning but not necessarily full puberty; he also uses the principle of ihtiyat (caution). The Sahibayn rely on the same hadith but interpret 'ajaza' as permission to fight, equating it with bulugh. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the Sahibayn's view of fifteen years, though individual practice may vary.

Key Terms
الْبُلُوغ (al-bulūgh): Legal adulthood with full capacity for worship and transactions.
الْخَبَل (al-khabal): Pregnancy, a clear sign of puberty for girls.
رَاهَقَ (rāhaqa): To approach puberty, i.e., be close to the age of bulugh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, at what age does a girl reach puberty if no physical signs appear?
A) 15 years
B) 17 years
C) 18 years
D) 19 years

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that puberty is reached at fifteen lunar years for both boys and girls, regardless of physical signs.

Conditions and Binding Nature of Confession (Iqrar)
States the conditions and binding effect of confession with multiple proof texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid confession in Hanafi fiqh and explain why a confession is binding even if the object is unknown.

Core Idea
A confession by a free, adult, sane person is binding, even if the object is unknown — clarification is required.

The Book of Confession (Kitab al-Iqrar) opens with a fundamental ruling: when a person meets the conditions of freedom, adulthood, and sanity, their acknowledgment of a right against themselves is binding. This lesson examines the conditions (shurut) and the binding effect (luzum) of confession, along with the textual proofs from Quran and Sunnah that establish this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is iqrar (confession) of a right (haqq) against oneself. The ruling is that it is binding (lazim) upon the confessor.
Binding means the confessor cannot revoke it and must fulfill the confessed right.
2
List the conditions for validity
The confessor must be free (hurr), adult (baligh), and sane ('aqil). Voluntariness (ikhtiyar) is also implied.
These conditions exclude the slave's confession (though it has separate rules), minor, insane, and coerced person.
3
Locate the proof texts
Four proofs are cited: Quran 3:81, hadith of the man confessing zina (Bukhari/Muslim), hadith of the lifted pen (Abu Dawud), and hadith of Ma'iz (Muslim).
Each proof supports the binding nature of confession from a different angle.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah for each proof
For each proof, identify the specific word or phrase that indicates the ruling. For example, 'أقررتم' in the verse implies binding acknowledgment; the Prophet's action in the hadiths shows acceptance of confession.
The hadith of the lifted pen establishes the requirement of sanity and adulthood.
5
Understand the ruling on the unknown object
The passage states that the confession is binding whether the object is known or unknown. If unknown, the confessor is told to clarify it. This is derived from the Ma'iz hadith where the Prophet asked for details.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from some other schools that may require the object to be known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الْإِقْرَارِ
قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللهُ - ل إذَا أَقَرَّ الحُرُ الْبَالِغُ الْعَاقِلُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِحَقِّ لَزِمَهُ إِقْرَارُهُ فَجُهُولًا كَانَ مَا أَقَرَّ بِهِ، أَوْ مَعْلُومًا وَيُقَالُ لَهُ: بَيِّنْ الْمَجْهُولَ
وجه: (1) الآية لثبوت إِذَا أَقَرَّ الْخُرُ الْبَالِغُ الْعَاقِلُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِحَقِّ لَزِمَهُ إِقْرَارُهُ \ قَالَ أَقْرَرْتُمْ وَأَخَذْتُمْ عَلَى ذَلِكُمْ إِصْرِى قَالُوا أَقْرَرْنَا قَالَ فَأَشْهَدُوا وَأَنَا مَعَكُم مِّنَ الشَّهِدِينَ (سورة ال عمران، 3أيت، نمبر 81)
وجه : (٢) الحديث لثبوت إذَا أَقَرَّ الْخَرُ الْبَالِغُ الْعَاقِلُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِحَقِّ لَزِمَهُ إِقْرَارُهُ \ عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ قَالَ: «أَتَى رَجُلٌ رَسُولَ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم وَهُوَ فِي الْمَسْجِدِ، فَنَادَاهُ فَقَالَ: يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ، إِنِّي زَنَيْتُ فَأَعْرَضَ عَنْهُ حَتَّى رَدَّدَ عَلَيْهِ أَرْبَعَ مَرَّاتٍ، فَلَمَّا شَهِدَ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ أَرْبَعَ شَهَادَاتٍ، دَعَاهُ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَالَ : أَبِكَ جُنُونٌ؟، قَالَ: لَا ، قَالَ فَهَلْ أَحْصَنْتَ؟، قَالَ: نَعَمْ، فَقَالَ النَّبِيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم : اذْهَبُوا بِهِ فَارْجُمُوهُ، (بخاري شريف، بَابٌ : لَا يُرْجَمُ الْمَجْنُونُ وَالْمَجْنُونَةُ، نمبر 6815/مسلم شريف: باب من اعترف على نفسه بالزنى، نمبر 1996)
وجه:(۳) الحديث لثبوت إذَا أَقَرَّ الْحُرُّ الْبَالِغُ الْعَاقِلُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِحَقِّ لَزِمَهُ إِقْرَارُهُ عَنْ عَائِشَةَ
"
أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: رُفِعَ الْقَلَمُ عَنْ ثَلَاثَةِ: عَنِ النَّائِم حَتَّى يَسْتَيْقِظَ، وَعَنِ الْمُبْتَلَى حَتَّى يَبْرَأَ وَعَنِ الصَّبِيِّ حَتَّى يَكْبُرَ (سنن ابوداود شريف، با فِي الْمَجْنُونِ يَسْرِقُ أَوْ يُصِيبُ حَدَّا، 4398)
وجه: (1) الحديث لثبوت وَيُقَالُ لَهُ: بَيِّنْ الْمَجْهُولَ \ قَالَ : جَاءَ مَاعِزُ بْنُ مَالِكِ إِلَى النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَالَ: يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ طَهَرْني. فَقَالَ (وَيْحَكَ ارْجِعْ فَاسْتَغْفِرِ اللَّهَ وَتُبْ إِلَيْهِ) قَالَ: فَرَجَعَ غَيْرَ بَعِيدٍ.
ثُمَّ جَاءَ فَقَالَ: يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ! طَهِّرْني..... حَتَّى إِذَا كَانَتِ الرَّابِعَةُ قَالَ لَهُ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ أطهرك؟) فقال: من الزنى/ ثُمَّ جَاءَتْهُ امْرَأَةٌ مِنْ عَامِدٍ مِنَ الْأَزْدِ. فَقَالَتْ: يَا رَسُولَ اللَّهِ! طَهِّرْني ..... قَالَ: (وَمَا ذَاكِ؟) قالت: إنها حبلى من الزنى. فَقَالَ (آنْتِ؟) قَالَتْ: نَعَمْ، مسلم شریف باب من اعترف على نفسه بالزنى، نمبر 1595)
اصول: آدمی آزاد، عاقل، بالغ اور باختیار ہے تو اپنے اوپر کسی چیز کا اقرار کر سکتا ہے، یہ اس کا ذاتی حق ہے۔
Book of Confession. He (the author) said - may Allah have mercy on him: When a free, adult, sane person makes a confession against himself of a right, his confession is binding upon him, whether what he confessed to is unknown or known. And he is told: clarify the unknown. Evidence (1): The verse for establishing: When a free, adult, sane person confesses against himself of a right, his confession is binding. He said: 'You have confessed and taken My covenant...' Evidence (2): The hadith... Evidence (3): The hadith... Evidence (1) for: And he is told: clarify the unknown. He said: Ma'iz bin Malik came to the Prophet... Principle: A person who is free, sane, adult, and voluntary can confess something against himself; this is his personal right.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage opens Kitab al-Iqrar (Book of Confession) with a general ruling. The author states that a confession made by a free, adult, sane person against himself regarding a right (haqq) is binding. This applies whether the object of confession is known or unknown; if unknown, he must clarify it. The hadith proofs include: (1) the verse from Surah Al-Imran where Allah takes a covenant from the prophets, which implies acceptance of confession; (2) the hadith of the man who confessed to zina four times before the Prophet, who then ordered his stoning after verifying his sanity and marital status; (3) the hadith 'The pen is lifted from three: the sleeping until he wakes, the afflicted until he recovers, and the child until he becomes adult' showing that sanity and adulthood are conditions; (4) the hadith of Ma'iz and the woman from Ghaymid who confessed to zina, illustrating that a confession is accepted and acted upon. The Urdu note summarizes: a person who is free, sane, adult, and voluntary can make a confession against himself—it is his personal right.

Key Terms
إقراار (iqrar): A statement by a person acknowledging a right (haqq) against himself in favor of another.
حُر (hurr): Free person (not a slave). In this context, a slave's confession may have different rules.
بَالِغ (baligh): Adult who has reached puberty (usually 15 lunar years or signs of puberty).
عَاقِل ('aqil): Sane person; not insane or mentally incapacitated.
لَزِمَهُ (lazimahu): It is binding upon him; the confession creates a legal obligation that cannot be retracted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is required for a confession to be binding?
A) The confessor must be free, adult, and sane.
B) The object of confession must be known immediately.
C) The confession must be witnessed by two people.
D) The confessor must be a male.

Key Takeaway
A confession made by a free, adult, sane person is binding even if the object is unknown, but clarification is required.

Rulings on Ambiguous Confessions and Minimum Amounts
Provides detailed case rulings for ambiguous confessions and establishes minimum values for certain expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for ambiguous confessions (e.g., 'shay', 'mal', 'darahim') and apply the minimum amounts derived from 'urf to determine the confessor's liability.

Core Idea
An ambiguous confession is interpreted by customary minima: 'mal' is any amount, 'mal 'azim' is at least 200 dirhams, 'dirhams' is three.

When someone says 'I owe you something' without specifying, the Hanafi school has established default interpretations based on the words used. These presumptions protect both parties from indefinite claims. The confessor's word is accepted with his oath, but the wording sets lower bounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous phrase
Determine which term the confessor used: shay', mal, mal 'azim, darahim kathirah, darahim, kadha kadha, or kadha wa kadha.
2
Recall the Hanafi minimum for that phrase
For each term, there is a specific minimum amount. Memorize the defaults: mal 'azim = 200, darahim kathirah = 10, darahim = 3, kadha kadha = 11, kadha wa kadha = 21.
These minima are based on 'urf (customary usage).
3
Apply the principle 'al-qawlu qawluhoo'
If the claimant demands more than the minimum, the confessor's word (with oath) is taken as true regarding the amount he admits. He is only bound to pay the minimum unless he voluntarily specifies more.
The hadith 'al-bayyinah 'ala man idda'a' supports that the burden is on the claimant.
4
Note the proofs
The passage cites the practice of Shuraih (qawl sahabi) and two hadiths to establish that the confessor's word is accepted and that his ambiguous statement is interpreted by 'urf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ه وَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مَالٌ فَالْمَرْجِعُ فِيهِ إِلَى بَيَانِهِ إِلَيْهِ وَيُقْبَلُ قَوْلُهُ: فِي الْقَلِيلِ، وَالْكَثِيرِ فَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مَالٌ عَظِيمٌ لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ مِائَتَيْ دِرْهَم فَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ دَرَاهِمُ كَثِيرَةٌ لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ عَشَرَةِ دَرَاهِمَ فَإِنْ قَالَ دَرَاهِمُ فَهِيَ ثَلَاثَةٌ إِلَّا أَنْ يُبَيِّنَ أَكْثَرَ مِنْهَا وَإِنْ قَالَ كَذَا كَذَا دِرْهَمَا لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ دِرْهَمَا وَإِنْ قَالَ كَذَا وَكَذَا دِرْهَمَا لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ أَحَدٍ وَعِشْرِينَ دِرْهَما
ل وَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مَالٌ فَالْمَرْجِعُ فِيهِ إِلَى بَيَانِهِ إِلَيْهِ وَيُقْبَلُ قَوْلُهُ: فِي الْقَلِيلِ، وَالْكَثِيرِ فَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مَالٌ عَظِيمٌ لَمْ يُصَدَّقُ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ مِائَتَيْ دِرْهَم وجه (۱) قول التابعى لثبوت فَإِنْ قَالَ: لِفُلَانٍ عَلَيَّ شَيْءٌ لَزِمَهُ أَنْ يُبَيِّنَ مَا لَهُ قِيمَةٌ أَعَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ النَّخَعِيُّ، أَنَّ رَجُلًا أَقَرَّ عِنْدَ شُرَيْحٍ ثُمَّ ذَهَبَ يُنْكِرُ ، فَقَالَ لَهُ شُرَيْحٌ : " شَهِدَ عَلَيْكَ ابْنُ أُخْتِ خَالَتِكَ " قَالَ: وَحَدَّثَنَا ابْنُ سِيرِينَ أَنَّ شُرَيْئًا قَالَ لَهُ: " شَهِدَ عَلَيْكَ ابْنُ أُخْتِ خَالَتِكَ " (السنن الكبري لليبيهقي، بَابُ مَنْ يَجُوزُ إِقْرَارُهُ، غبر 11452)
د وجه : (١) الحديث لثبوت وَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُ: فِيهِ مَعَ يَمِينِهِ إِنْ ادَّعَى الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ \ عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ ، أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللهِ ، قَالَ: «الْبَيِّنَةُ عَلَى مَن ادَّعَى ، وَالْيَمِينُ عَلَى مَنْ أَنْكَرَ إِلَّا فِي الْقَسَامَةِ» (سنن دارقطني ، فِي الْمَرْأَةِ تُقْتَلُ إِذَا ارْتَدَّتْ ،نمبر 4507)
وجه : (۲) الحديث لثبوت وَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُ: فِيهِ مَعَ يَمِينِهِ إِنْ ادَّعَى الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ اعَنْ ابْنِ عَبَّاسِ؛ أَن النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ لَوْ يُعْطَى النَّاسُ بِدَعْوَاهُمْ لادَّعَى نَاسٌ دِمَاءَ رِجَالٍ وَأَمْوَاهُمْ. ولكن اليمين على المدعى عليه) » (مسلم) شريف ، بَاب الْيَمِينِ عَلَى الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ، نمبر 1711)
د. وجه: (1) الحديث لثبوت وَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مَالٌ فَالْمَرْجِعُ فِيهِ إِلَى بَيَانِهِ إِلَيْهِ \ عَنْ سُرَّقٍ قَالَ : كَانَ لِرَجُل مَالٌ عَلَيَّ ، أَوْ قَالَ: عَلَيَّ دَيْنٌ ، فَذَهَبَ فِي إِلَى رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَلَمْ يُصِبْ لِي مَالًا بي ، «فَبَاعَنِي مِنْهُ ، أَوْ بَاعَنِي لَهُ » » سنن دارقطني كِتَابُ الْبُيُوع، نمبر 3025)
اصول: عربی میں لفظ علی لزوم کے لئے آتا ہے، لہذا جب علی کا جملہ کہا تو قرض سمجھا جائے گاجو کہ لازم ہو گا۔
لغات: السَّمَانُعُ : وہ مال جسے لوگ لینا پسند نہ کرے ، تَعْوِيلًا : اعتماد کرنا، يُتَمَوَّلُ : مال دار بنتا۔
If he says: 'So-and-so has something due from me,' he must specify something of value. And his word is accepted regarding it, along with his oath, if the claimant claims more than that. And if he says: 'He has wealth due from me,' the reference is to his own clarification, and his word is accepted for both little and much. If he says: 'He has great wealth due from me,' he is not believed for less than two hundred dirhams. If he says: 'He has many dirhams due from me,' he is not believed for less than ten dirhams. If he says: 'Dirhams,' they are three unless he specifies more. If he says: 'Such-and-such dirham,' he is not believed for less than eleven dirhams. If he says: 'Such-and-such and such-and-such dirham,' he is not believed for less than twenty-one dirhams. And if he says: 'He has wealth due from me,' the reference is to his own clarification, and his word is accepted for both little and much. If he says: 'He has great wealth due from me,' he is not believed for less than two hundred dirhams. Evidence (1): Statement of the Tabi'i... Evidence (1) hadith: 'Proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' Evidence (2) hadith: 'If people were given based on their claims...' Evidence (1) hadith: about a man who said 'I have a debt'... Principle: In Arabic, the word 'علي' (upon) indicates obligation, so when a sentence with 'على' is said, it is considered a debt that is binding. Vocabulary: السَّمَانُعُ: wealth that people dislike to take; تَعْوِيلًا: reliance; يُتَمَوَّلُ: becomes wealthy.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage details how ambiguous confessions are interpreted. If a person says 'I owe something' (shay'), he must specify an item of value. The confessor's word is accepted with his oath if the claimant demands more. Different words imply different minima: 'mal' (wealth) is accepted as any amount; 'mal 'azim' (great wealth) requires at least 200 dirhams; 'darahim kathirah' (many dirhams) at least 10; 'darahim' (dirhams) at least 3 unless more is stated; 'kadha kadha dirham' implies at least 11; 'kadha wa kadha dirham' at least 21. The proofs include: (1) the practice of the judge Shuraih who held a confessor to his admission by a witty remark; (2) the famous hadith 'Al-bayyina 'ala man idda'a' (the burden of proof is on the claimant, the oath on the denier); (3) the hadith 'If people were given based on their claims, they would claim lives and wealth'; (4) a hadith about a man who came to the Prophet regarding a debt, showing that the word of the debtor is accepted. The Urdu note adds that the word 'ala' (upon) always implies a debt, so the confession is binding.

Key Terms
شَيْء (shay'): Something; an unspecified object that must have value (qimah) in the context of confession.
مَال (mal): Wealth or property; a broad term that includes dirhams, goods, etc. In this passage, it is accepted as any amount unless qualified as 'azim'.
دِرْهَم (dirham): A silver coin; unit of currency. Used as the standard measure for minimum amounts.
مَالٌ عَظِيم (malun 'azim): Great wealth; interpreted as at least 200 dirhams in this context.
الْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُ (al-qawlu qawluhoo): His word is accepted; i.e., the confessor's statement is taken as true regarding the amount he owes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ambiguous confessions are interpreted by customary minima, with the confessor's word accepted under oath, preventing excessive claims.

Vague Iqrar: Minimum Amounts in Hanafi Law
Al-Quduri's matn on vague acknowledgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum amounts of debt that are binding upon a person who makes a vague acknowledgment in Hanafi law.

Core Idea
Vague acknowledgments default to fixed minimums to prevent abuse — each term has a specific threshold.

In Hanafi fiqh, when someone acknowledges a debt using vague language, the law prescribes default minimums to protect the creditor. This lesson from Al-Quduri's matn covers six common vague expressions and their legal interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making a vague iqrar (acknowledgment) of a debt. The ruling: the debtor's own clarification is accepted with an oath, but certain vague terms trigger fixed minimum amounts.
2
List the six vague expressions
From the passage: 'shay' (something), 'mal' (wealth), 'mal 'azim' (great wealth), 'darahim kathira' (many dirhams), 'darahim' (dirhams), 'kadha kadha' (such and such), and 'kadha wa kadha' (such and such combined).
Also includes the general rule for 'mal'.
3
State the minimum for each
For each expression, recall the minimum amount: 'shay' requires an item of value; 'mal' any amount; 'mal 'azim' at least 200 dirhams; 'darahim kathira' at least 10; 'darahim' at least 3; 'kadha kadha' at least 11 dirhams; 'kadha wa kadha' at least 21 dirhams.
These are fixed defaults; the debtor cannot claim less.
4
Understand the rationale
These minimums are based on customary usage (urf) and the nisab of zakat (200 dirhams for great wealth). They prevent the debtor from exploiting vagueness to minimize liability.
5
Apply to a hypothetical
Example: If someone says 'I owe you many dirhams', a Hanafi judge will presume at least 10 dirhams unless the debtor proves otherwise with an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قَالَ: لِفُلَانٍ عَلَيَّ شَيْءٌ لَزِمَهُ أَنْ يُبَيِّنَ مَا لَهُ قِيمَةٌ ، وَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُ: فِيهِ مَعَ يَمِينِهِ إِنْ ادَّعَى الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ أَكْثَرَ مِنْ ذَلِكَ وَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مَالٌ فَالْمَرْجِعُ فِيهِ إِلَى بَيَانِهِ إِلَيْهِ وَيُقْبَلُ قَوْلُهُ: فِي الْقَلِيلِ، وَالْكَثِيرِ فَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مَالٌ عَظِيمٌ لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ مِائَتَيْ دِرْهَم فَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ دَرَاهِمُ كَثِيرَةٌ لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ عَشَرَةِ دَرَاهِمَ فَإِنْ قَالَ دَرَاهِمُ فَهِيَ ثَلَاثَةٌ إِلَّا أَنْ يُبَيِّنَ أَكْثَرَ مِنْهَا وَإِنْ قَالَ كَذَا كَذَا دِرْهَمَا لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ دِرْهَمَا وَإِنْ قَالَ كَذَا وَكَذَا دِرْهَمَا لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ فِي أَقَلَّ مِنْ أَحَدٍ وَعِشْرِينَ دِرْهَما
If he said: 'I owe something to so-and-so', he must specify something of value, and his word is accepted regarding it with his oath if the beneficiary claims more than that. And if he said: 'I owe him wealth', then reference is to his explanation, and his word is accepted in little and much. If he said: 'I owe him great wealth', he is not believed in less than two hundred dirhams. If he said: 'I owe him many dirhams', he is not believed in less than ten dirhams. If he said: 'dirhams', then it is three unless he specifies more than that. If he said: 'kadha kadha dirhaman', he is not believed in less than eleven dirhams. If he said: 'kadha wa kadha dirhaman', he is not believed in less than twenty-one dirhams.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's matn on confessions (iqrar) sets out how vague statements of debt are interpreted. The main principle: when someone acknowledges a debt in vague terms, his own clarification is accepted with an oath if the creditor claims more. However, certain terms have fixed minimums to prevent abuse. 'Something' (shay’) requires specifying an item of value. 'Wealth' (mal) is accepted at any amount. 'Great wealth' (mal 'azim) defaults to at least 200 dirhams, which is the nisab for zakat. 'Many dirhams' defaults to at least 10. 'Dirhams' alone means 3, the plural minimum. 'Kadha kadha' (such and such) implies 11, and 'kadha wa kadha' implies 21, as these are common expressions for numbers in the tens and twenties. These minimums protect the creditor from the debtor claiming an unrealistically low amount after having used a vague term.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): An acknowledgment of a right or debt owed to another, which creates a binding obligation.
دِرْهَم (dirham): A silver coin, the standard unit of currency in classical Islamic law.
مَالٌ عَظِيمٌ (mal 'azim): Literally 'great wealth'; in Hanafi iqrar, it is interpreted as at least 200 dirhams.
دَرَاهِمُ كَثِيرَةٌ (darahim kathira): Many dirhams; in Hanafi iqrar, interpreted as at least 10 dirhams.
كَذَا كَذَا (kadha kadha): Such and such; a vague expression for a number; in Hanafi iqrar, interpreted as at least 11 dirhams.
كَذَا وَكَذَا (kadha wa kadha): Such and such combined; a vague expression for a number; in Hanafi iqrar, interpreted as at least 21 dirhams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'I owe him many dirhams', what is the minimum amount that is binding on him?
A) 1 dirham
B) 3 dirhams
C) 10 dirhams
D) 200 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi iqrar, vague terms have fixed minimums: 'something' requires value, 'wealth' any amount, 'great wealth' 200 dirhams, 'many dirhams' 10, 'dirhams' 3, 'kadha kadha' 11, and 'kadha wa kadha' 21.

Expressions and Responses in Iqrar
Rulings on verbal formulations that create legal acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which prepositions indicate debt vs trust in iqrar and state which responses constitute a valid acknowledgment.

Core Idea
The choice of preposition determines whether you acknowledge a debt or a trust; certain replies to a claim are legal admissions.

In Islamic law, an acknowledgment (iqrar) is a binding declaration. The precise wording matters greatly: saying 'I owe him' creates a debt, while 'I have with me his property' creates a trust. Even replies like 'Weigh it' can be taken as admissions. Let's examine how Hanafi fiqh interprets these expressions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish debt from trust prepositions
Identify the two prepositions for debt ('alayya, qibali) and two for trust ('indi, ma'i). Understand the legal consequence: debt requires repayment, trust requires return of property.
The key difference is ownership: debt is owed by the person, trust is held on behalf.
2
Recognize responses that constitute iqrar
Consider the four responses listed: 'weigh it', 'count it', 'grant me a delay', 'I have paid you'. Each implies the speaker accepts the existence of the debt.
'I have paid you' acknowledges the original debt even while claiming discharge.
3
Apply the rules to a new scenario
Given a statement like 'I have your money in my safe', determine whether it is an iqrar of debt or trust.
Use the preposition test: 'with me' (ma'i) indicates trust.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ، أَوْ قِبَلِي فَقَدْ أَقَرَّ بِدَيْنِ ووَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عِنْدِي، أَوْ مَعِي فَهُوَ إقْرَارٌ بِأَمَانَةٍ فِي يَدِهِ وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ رَجُلٌ: لِي عَلَيْكَ أَلْفُ دِرْهَم قَالَ: اتَّزِنْهَا أَوْ انْتَقِدْهَا، أَوْ أَجِلْنِيهَا، أَوْ قَدْ قَضَيْتُكَهَا فَهُوَ إقْرَارٌ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِدَيْنِ مُؤَجَّلٍ فَصَدَّقَهُ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ فِي الدَّيْنِ وَكَذَّبَهُ فِي الْأَجَلِ لَزِمَهُ الدَّيْنُ حَالَّا وَيُسْتَخْلَفُ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ فِي الْأَجَلِ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِدَيْنِ وَاسْتَغْنَى مُتَّصِلًا بِإِقْرَارِهِ صَحَ الِاسْتِثْنَاءُ وَلَزِمَهُ الْبَاقِي وَسَوَاءٌ الْأَقَلُ، أَوْ الْأَكْثَرُ وَإِنْ اسْتَغْنَى الْجَمِيعَ لَزِمَهُ الْإِقْرَارُ وَبَطَلَ الِاسْتِثْنَاءُ فَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مِائَةُ دِرْهَمٍ إِلَّا دِينَارًا، أَوْ إِلَّا قَفِينَ حِنْطَةٍ لَزِمَهُ الْمِائَةُ الدِّرْهَمِ إِلَّا قِيمَةَ الدِّينَارِ، أَوْ الْقَفِيزِ قَوْلُهُ: وَلَوْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مِائَةٌ وَدِرْهَمْ فَالْمِانَةُ دَرَاهِمُ وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ مِائَةٌ وَثَوْبٌ لَزِمَهُ ثَوْبٌ وَاحِدٌ
If he said: 'I owe him' or 'it is incumbent on me', then he has acknowledged a debt. And if he said: 'I have with him' or 'with me', then it is an acknowledgment of a trust in his hand. And if a man said to him: 'I have a thousand dirhams due from you' and he said: 'Weigh it, or count it, or grant me a delay, or I have paid you', then it is an acknowledgment. And whoever acknowledged a deferred debt, and the beneficiary confirmed the debt but denied the deferment, the debt becomes immediately due, and the beneficiary is made to take an oath regarding the deferment. And whoever acknowledged a debt and made an exception connected to his acknowledgment, the exception is valid and the remainder is binding, whether little or much. But if he excepted the entire amount, the acknowledgment is binding and the exception is void. If he said: 'I owe him a hundred dirhams except a dinar, or except a qafiz of wheat', then he owes the hundred dirhams minus the value of the dinar or qafiz. His statement: and if he said: 'I owe him a hundred and a dirham', then the hundred is dirhams. And if he said: 'I owe him a hundred and a garment', then he owes one garment.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage covers several important rules. First, the preposition used in iqrar matters: 'alayya' and 'qibali' indicate a debt (dayn), while 'indi' and 'ma'i' indicate a trust (amanah) in one's possession. Second, certain responses to a claim constitute acknowledgment, such as asking to weigh the money, asking for a delay, or claiming to have paid — these imply the debt is accepted. Third, if someone acknowledges a deferred debt and the creditor agrees it's a debt but disputes the deferment, the debt becomes immediately due (hallan) and the creditor must swear that there was no deferment. Fourth, exceptions (istithna') connected to the acknowledgment are valid for part of the debt but void if they exclude the entire amount; the remaining part is owed. Fifth, if the debt is of different types (e.g., dirhams and a dinar, or dirhams and wheat), the exception is calculated by value. Finally, if someone says 'a hundred and a dirham', the 'hundred' is interpreted as dirhams (i.e., 101 dirhams), but if he says 'a hundred and a garment', he only owes one garment (the hundred is ambiguous).

Key Terms
أمانة (amanah): A trust; property held by one person on behalf of another, which must be returned.
مؤجل (mu'ajjal): A debt with a deferred payment term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'I have with me' (معي) something, what type of iqrar is it?
A) Iqrar of debt
B) Iqrar of trust
C) No iqrar at all
D) Iqrar of both debt and trust

Key Takeaway
The preposition used in iqrar determines whether you acknowledge a debt or a trust; certain replies are admissions.

Exceptions and Deferred Debts in Iqrar
Rulings on exceptions and deferred debts in iqrar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for valid exceptions in iqrar and resolve disputes over deferred debts.

Core Idea
An exception must be connected and cannot exclude the entire amount; a disputed deferment makes the debt immediately due.

When someone acknowledges a debt but adds an exception, the exception is only valid if it is connected and does not swallow the whole. If the creditor claims the debt was deferred but the debtor denies, the debt becomes payable immediately. This lesson unpacks the details of these two scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Understand connected exception (musttassil)
The exception must be spoken immediately after the iqrar without interruption. If it is separated, the exception is invalid and the full amount is due.
This is the Hanafi condition of ittisal.
2
Apply the rule that total exception is void
If someone says 'I owe you 100 dirhams except 100 dirhams', the exception is invalid; he owes the full 100. Exception cannot nullify the entire acknowledgment.
This prevents repudiation of the iqrar itself.
3
Handle mixed asset exceptions
If the debt is in dirhams and the exception is in a different commodity (e.g., a dinar), convert the exception to its value and subtract from the debt.
The conversion is at market value.
4
Resolve deferred debt dispute
If the debtor acknowledges a deferred debt but the creditor denies the deferment, the debt becomes immediately due (hālan) and the creditor must swear that there was no deferment.
The oath is on the creditor's claim of no deferment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ، أَوْ قِبَلِي فَقَدْ أَقَرَّ بِدَيْنِ ووَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عِنْدِي، أَوْ مَعِي فَهُوَ إقْرَارٌ بِأَمَانَةٍ فِي يَدِهِ وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ رَجُلٌ: لِي عَلَيْكَ أَلْفُ دِرْهَم قَالَ: اتَّزِنْهَا أَوْ انْتَقِدْهَا، أَوْ أَجِلْنِيهَا، أَوْ قَدْ قَضَيْتُكَهَا فَهُوَ إقْرَارٌ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِدَيْنِ مُؤَجَّلٍ فَصَدَّقَهُ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ فِي الدَّيْنِ وَكَذَّبَهُ فِي الْأَجَلِ لَزِمَهُ الدَّيْنُ حَالَّا وَيُسْتَخْلَفُ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ فِي الْأَجَلِ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِدَيْنِ وَاسْتَغْنَى مُتَّصِلًا بِإِقْرَارِهِ صَحَ الِاسْتِثْنَاءُ وَلَزِمَهُ الْبَاقِي وَسَوَاءٌ الْأَقَلُ، أَوْ الْأَكْثَرُ وَإِنْ اسْتَغْنَى الْجَمِيعَ لَزِمَهُ الْإِقْرَارُ وَبَطَلَ الِاسْتِثْنَاءُ فَإِنْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مِائَةُ دِرْهَمٍ إِلَّا دِينَارًا، أَوْ إِلَّا قَفِينَ حِنْطَةٍ لَزِمَهُ الْمِائَةُ الدِّرْهَمِ إِلَّا قِيمَةَ الدِّينَارِ، أَوْ الْقَفِيزِ قَوْلُهُ: وَلَوْ قَالَ: لَهُ عَلَيَّ مِائَةٌ وَدِرْهَمْ فَالْمِانَةُ دَرَاهِمُ وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ مِائَةٌ وَثَوْبٌ لَزِمَهُ ثَوْبٌ وَاحِدٌ
If he said: 'I owe him' or 'it is incumbent on me', then he has acknowledged a debt. And if he said: 'I have with him' or 'with me', then it is an acknowledgment of a trust in his hand. And if a man said to him: 'I have a thousand dirhams due from you' and he said: 'Weigh it, or count it, or grant me a delay, or I have paid you', then it is an acknowledgment. And whoever acknowledged a deferred debt, and the beneficiary confirmed the debt but denied the deferment, the debt becomes immediately due, and the beneficiary is made to take an oath regarding the deferment. مؤجل (mu'ajjal): A debt with a deferred payment term.
استثناء (istithna'): An exception made in an acknowledgment, excluding a portion of the debt.

Ruling on Iqrar with 'In Sha Allah'
Ruling on the effect of saying 'in sha Allah' in iqrar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a person says 'in sha Allah' immediately after an acknowledgment and explain why it prevents the iqrar from being binding.

Core Idea
Iqrar with 'in sha Allah' connected is like an oath exception — it suspends the binding effect.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: someone acknowledges a debt but adds 'if Allah wills.' Does this make the acknowledgment non-binding? The Hanafi school treats it as a suspensive condition, similar to saying 'I swear, if Allah wills.' The ruling is clear: the iqrar does not bind him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is an acknowledgment (iqrar) of something (e.g., debt). The condition is the utterance of 'in sha Allah' immediately after (mutassilan).
The phrase must be connected; if separate, different ruling.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The acknowledgment does not bind him (lam yalzamhu). He is not compelled to fulfill it.
This is the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah).
3
Understand the analogy
The ruling is based on analogy to the exception in oaths (istithna' fi al-yamin). Just as saying 'in sha Allah' in an oath negates the obligation to expiate, here it negates the binding effect of iqrar.
Connection (ittisal) is essential.
4
Note the practical consequence
If someone later denies the acknowledgment, the iqrar cannot be enforced against him.
The beneficiary cannot claim based on such iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِشَيْءٍ وَقَالَ: إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مُتَّصِلًا بِإِقْرَارِهِ لَمْ يَلْزَمْهُ الْإِقْرَارُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ وَشَرَطَ الْخِيَارَ لِنَفْسِهِ لَزِمَهُ الْإِقْرَارُ وَبَطَلَ الْخِيَارُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِدَارٍ وَاسْتَغْنَى بِنَاءَهَا لِنَفْسِهِ فَلِلْمُقَرِ لَهُ الدَّارُ، وَالْبِنَاءُ وَإِنْ قَالَ بِنَاءُ هَذِهِ الدَّارِ لِي، وَالْعَرْصَةُ لِفُلَانٍ فَهُوَ كَمَا قَالَ
And whoever acknowledged something and said: 'If Allah wills' connected to his acknowledgment, the acknowledgment does not bind him. And whoever acknowledged and stipulated the option (khiyar) for himself, the acknowledgment is binding and the option is void. And whoever acknowledged a house and excepted its building for himself, then the house and the building belong to the beneficiary. If he said: 'The building of this house is mine, and the land is for so-and-so', then it is as he said.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage deals with conditions attached to an acknowledgment. First, if a person says 'in sha Allah' (God willing) immediately after the iqrar, it suspends the obligation because it indicates hesitancy, similar to its effect in oaths (yamin). However, if the suspensive clause is not connected, it may be treated differently. Second, if someone acknowledges a debt but stipulates an option to revoke (khiyar), the option is invalid and the iqrar is binding because iqrar is a unilateral binding act; it does not allow for a right of rescission. Third, regarding immovable property: if someone acknowledges that a house belongs to another but excepts the building (the structure) for himself, the entire house (including building) goes to the beneficiary because the building is part of the house. But if he explicitly says the building is his and the land is for the other, then his statement is accepted as an acknowledgment of two separate properties.

Key Terms
إن شاء الله (in sha Allah): A conditional phrase meaning 'if Allah wills'; in iqrar, it prevents the acknowledgment from being binding if connected.
إقرار (iqrar): An acknowledgment or admission of a right or obligation; it is a binding unilateral statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for 'in sha Allah' to prevent binding in iqrar?
A) It must be said silently.
B) It must be said immediately and connected to the iqrar.
C) It must be said in Arabic.
D) It must be witnessed.

Key Takeaway
An iqrar is not binding if the person says 'in sha Allah' immediately after.

Ruling on Iqrar with Khiyar Option
Ruling on stipulating an option in iqrar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when someone stipulates an option to rescind an acknowledgment and explain why the option is void.

Core Idea
Iqrar is a unilateral binding act; stipulating an option is invalid and the iqrar stands.

Sometimes a person acknowledges a right but adds that he has the option to cancel it. This lesson clarifies that iqrar does not admit options like contracts. The acknowledgment is binding immediately, and the condition is void.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the additional stipulation
The act is iqrar (acknowledgment). The stipulation is khiyar (option) for oneself, e.g., 'I acknowledge the debt but I have the option to cancel.'
The option can be for a fixed period or indefinite.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The iqrar is binding (lazim) and the option is void (batala).
This is because iqrar is a binding statement, not a contract subject to options.
3
Understand the basis
Iqrar is an akhbar (report) that creates a right for the beneficiary. Once made, it is conclusive; the person cannot revoke it.
Contracts have khiyar because they require mutual consent; iqrar is unilateral.
4
Practical implication
The beneficiary can immediately claim the right, and the person cannot opt out.
If the person later refuses, the court enforces the iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِشَيْءٍ وَقَالَ: إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مُتَّصِلًا بِإِقْرَارِهِ لَمْ يَلْزَمْهُ الْإِقْرَارُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ وَشَرَطَ الْخِيَارَ لِنَفْسِهِ لَزِمَهُ الْإِقْرَارُ وَبَطَلَ الْخِيَارُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِدَارٍ وَاسْتَغْنَى بِنَاءَهَا لِنَفْسِهِ فَلِلْمُقَرِ لَهُ الدَّارُ، وَالْبِنَاءُ وَإِنْ قَالَ بِنَاءُ هَذِهِ الدَّارِ لِي، وَالْعَرْصَةُ لِفُلَانٍ فَهُوَ كَمَا قَالَ
And whoever acknowledged something and said: 'If Allah wills' connected to his acknowledgment, the acknowledgment does not bind him. And whoever acknowledged and stipulated the option (khiyar) for himself, the acknowledgment is binding and the option is void. And whoever acknowledged a house and excepted its building for himself, then the house and the building belong to the beneficiary. If he said: 'The building of this house is mine, and the land is for so-and-so', then it is as he said.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage deals with conditions attached to an acknowledgment. First, if a person says 'in sha Allah' (God willing) immediately after the iqrar, it suspends the obligation because it indicates hesitancy, similar to its effect in oaths (yamin). However, if the suspensive clause is not connected, it may be treated differently. Second, if someone acknowledges a debt but stipulates an option to revoke (khiyar), the option is invalid and the iqrar is binding because iqrar is a unilateral binding act; it does not allow for a right of rescission. But if he explicitly says the building is his and the land is for the other, then his statement is accepted as an acknowledgment of two separate properties.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): An option or right to rescind a contract; in iqrar, it is void.
إقرار (iqrar): An acknowledgment or admission; it is binding once made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ruling on Iqrar with Building Exception
Ruling on excepting the building in an iqrar involving a house.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scenarios of excepting a building in an iqrar of a house and state the correct ruling for each.

Core Idea
If you acknowledge a house and except its building, the exception is ineffective unless you clearly separate building and land.

When a person acknowledges that a house belongs to someone else but says the building is his, the default ruling is that the entire property (house, including building and land) goes to the beneficiary. However, if the person explicitly separates the building and land in his statement, then his exception is accepted. This lesson clarifies the difference.

Guided Path
1
First scenario: iqrar of house with general exception of building
He says: 'This house belongs to X, but the building is mine.' The iqrar is for the whole house, and the exception is invalid because the building is part of the house.
The term 'dar' includes both structure and land.
2
Ruling for first scenario
The house and building both go to the beneficiary (the person in whose favor the iqrar was made).
The exception is disregarded.
3
Second scenario: iqrar with explicit separation
He says: 'The building of this house is mine, and the land is for so-and-so.' Here he acknowledges two separate items.
Building and land are treated independently.
4
Ruling for second scenario
His statement is accepted as true: he keeps the building, and the land goes to the named person.
Because he disclaimed ownership of the land for himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِشَيْءٍ وَقَالَ: إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ مُتَّصِلًا بِإِقْرَارِهِ لَمْ يَلْزَمْهُ الْإِقْرَارُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ وَشَرَطَ الْخِيَارَ لِنَفْسِهِ لَزِمَهُ الْإِقْرَارُ وَبَطَلَ الْخِيَارُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِدَارٍ وَاسْتَغْنَى بِنَاءَهَا لِنَفْسِهِ فَلِلْمُقَرِ لَهُ الدَّارُ، وَالْبِنَاءُ وَإِنْ قَالَ بِنَاءُ هَذِهِ الدَّارِ لِي، وَالْعَرْصَةُ لِفُلَانٍ فَهُوَ كَمَا قَالَ
And whoever acknowledged something and said: 'If Allah wills' connected to his acknowledgment, the acknowledgment does not bind him. And whoever acknowledged and stipulated the option (khiyar) for himself, the acknowledgment is binding and the option is void. And whoever acknowledged a house and excepted its building for himself, then the house and the building belong to the beneficiary. If he said: 'The building of this house is mine, and the land is for so-and-so', then it is as he said.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage deals with conditions attached to an acknowledgment. First, if a person says 'in sha Allah' (God willing) immediately after the iqrar, it suspends the obligation because it indicates hesitancy, similar to its effect in oaths (yamin). However, if the suspensive clause is not connected, it may be treated differently. Second, if someone acknowledges a debt but stipulates an option to revoke (khiyar), the option is invalid and the iqrar is binding because iqrar is a unilateral binding act; it does not allow for a right of rescission. Third, regarding immovable property: if someone acknowledges that a house belongs to another but excepts the building (the structure) for himself, the entire house (including building) goes to the beneficiary because the building is part of the house. But if he explicitly says the building is his and the land is for the other, then his statement is accepted as an acknowledgment of two separate properties.

Key Terms
دار (dar): A house, including the land (arsah) and building (bina').
بِناء (bina'): Building, structure; the constructed part of a property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is an exception of the building in an iqrar of a house valid?
A) Always valid.
B) Never valid.
C) Valid only if the person explicitly says the building is his and the land belongs to someone else.
D) Valid if the building is separate from the land.

Key Takeaway
An exception of the building in an iqrar of a house is only valid if the building and land are explicitly separated in the statement.

Confession Involving Container and Content
Rulings on confession when object is described inside a container

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings on confession involving a container, and apply the Hanafi principle distinguishing movable from fixed containers.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a confession to an item in a movable container includes the container; a fixed container is not included.

This passage from Kitab al-Iqrar presents two clear rulings. They illustrate a key Hanafi principle: when a container (ẓarf) can be transferred with its content, it is owed; if it is fixed and cannot accompany the content, it is not. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion in legal confessions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Note the two scenarios: (1) dates in a basket, (2) an animal in a stable.
2
State the ruling for each case
For the basket, both dates and basket are binding. For the stable, only the animal is binding.
3
Identify the underlying distinction
The basket (qawṣarrah) is a movable container that can be delivered with its content; the stable (iṣṭabl) is a fixed structure that cannot be transferred.
This is the key legal principle.
4
Apply the principle to new examples
Consider other containers: a bag, a box, a house. Determine if they are movable or fixed.
The principle is general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِتَمْرٍ فِي قَوْصَرَّةٍ لَزِمَهُ التَّمْرُ، وَالْقَوْصَرَةُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِدَابَّةٍ فِي إِصْطَبْلٍ لَزِمَهُ الدَّابَّةُ خَاصَّةً
And whoever confesses to dates in a basket, the dates and the basket are binding. And whoever confesses to an animal in a stable, only the animal is binding.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Iqrar presents two rulings on confession involving a container (ẓarf) and its content (maẓrūf). In the first case, dates in a basket (qawṣarrah) – the basket is a container that can be moved with its content, so both are owed. In the second case, an animal in a stable (iṣṭabl) – the stable is a fixed structure that does not accompany the animal, so only the animal is owed. The underlying principle is: if the container is transferable with the content, it is included; otherwise, not.

Key Terms
قوصرة (qawṣarrah): A basket made of palm leaves used for dates.
إصطبل (iṣṭabl): A stable for animals, a fixed structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Horse only
B) Horse and stable
C) Stable only
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
A confession to an item in a movable container includes the container; a fixed container is not included.

Confession of a Contained Object: The Cloth-in-Ten-Cloths Dispute
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on container-confession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on confessing to an object inside another object, and distinguish between Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad al-Shaybani's positions regarding uncertain containers.

Core Idea
When confessed item is inside another, specificity of container determines liability; uncertainty limits it to one.

This passage examines a nuanced case in Hanafi fiqh: what happens when a person confesses to owing or usurping an item described as being inside another? The first two examples are straightforward—both container and content are owed. The third introduces a dispute between the two great disciples of Abu Hanifa: does 'a cloth in ten cloths' obligate eleven cloths or just one? Understanding this difference reveals how Hanafi jurists balance literal wording against reasonable interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the wording
The act is a confession of usurpation or debt. The wording describes a relationship: one item is inside another. Note the three patterns: (1) cloth in a handkerchief, (2) cloth in a cloth, (3) cloth in ten cloths.
The container is a specific item in first two, but a plurality (ten) in the third.
2
Distinguish specific vs. uncertain containers
In the first two cases, the container is a single identified item (handkerchief, another cloth). In the third, 'ten cloths' denotes an indefinite number—does it mean ten specific cloths or a vague group? This ambiguity triggers the ikhtilaf.
Abu Yusuf treats 'in ten cloths' as an uncertain descriptor; Muhammad takes it literally.
3
State the two intra-Hanafi positions
Abu Yusuf holds the confessor owes only one cloth (the content) because the container phrasing is uncertain and thus not binding. Muhammad holds all eleven (ten plus the content) are owed, taking the confession at face value.
Both positions are recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Marghinani) prefer Abu Yusuf's view as it avoids imposing excessive liability and aligns with the principle that ambiguous wording is interpreted in favor of the confessor.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ غَصَبْته ثَوْبًا فِي مِنْدِيلِ لَزِمَاهُ جَمِيعًا وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ ثَوْبٌ فِي ثَوْبِ لَزِمَاهُ جَمِيعًا وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ ثَوْبٌ فِي عَشَرَةِ أَثْوَابِ لَمْ يَلْزَمْهُ عِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ إِلَّا ثَوْبٌ وَاحِدٌ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ: يَلْزَمُهُ أَحَدَ عَشَرَ ثَوْبًا
And if he says, 'I usurped a cloth in a handkerchief,' both are binding. And if he says, 'I owe you a cloth in a cloth,' both are binding. And if he says, 'I owe you a cloth in ten cloths,' according to Abu Yusuf nothing is binding except one cloth, and Muhammad said: eleven cloths are binding.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage addresses confession when the object is specified as being inside another object. In the first two examples, the container is specific (a handkerchief, another cloth), so both the container and content are owed. When the container is described as 'ten cloths', there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that only one cloth (the content) is binding because the container is uncertain; Muhammad holds that all ten plus the content (eleven) are binding because the confession is taken literally. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to later Hanafi scholars is Abu Yusuf's view, as it avoids imposing excessive liability.

Key Terms
منديل (mindīl): Handkerchief or small cloth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone confesses 'I owe you a cloth in ten cloths', how many cloths does he owe according to the mufta bihi?
A) One cloth
B) Ten cloths
C) Eleven cloths
D) None (confession invalid)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when the container is described with an indefinite number, the credited amount is only the content (Abu Yusuf's view).

Confession with Defective Item
Ruling on confession of usurpation when the item is defective or coins are counterfeit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi rule that a confessor's claim about the defect of the usurped item is accepted with his oath.

Core Idea
The confessor's word with oath prevails when he claims the item is defective.

This lesson examines a scenario where a person confesses to usurping an item (ghaṣb) but upon returning it, claims it is defective or that the coins are counterfeit. The Hanafi ruling gives weight to the confessor's statement, supported by an oath, to protect him from being forced to pay for a sound item beyond what he admitted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person confesses to usurping a cloth (or coins) and later brings it back, claiming it is defective (maʿīb) or that the coins are counterfeit (zuyūf).
The confession itself is the basis of liability.
2
State the ruling for the cloth
The text says: "al-qawl qawluhu maʿa yamīnihi" — his statement (about the defect) is accepted with his oath.
He does not need to prove the defect; his oath suffices.
3
Apply to the coins case
The same ruling applies if he confesses to dirhams and then says they are counterfeit.
This shows a general principle in Hanafi law.
4
Understand the legal effect
The confessor is liable only for the defective item as he described, not for a sound one. The burden shifts to the claimant to dispute the defect.
This prevents injustice against the confessor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِغَصْبٍ ثَوْبِ وَجَاءَ بِثَوْبِ مَعِيبٍ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهُ: مَعَ يَمِينِهِ وَكَذَا لَوْ أَقَرَّ بِدَرَاهِمَ وَقَالَ: هِيَ زُيُوفٌ
And whoever confesses to usurping a cloth and brings a defective cloth, the saying is his saying with his oath. Similarly if he confesses to dirhams and says: they are counterfeit.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This ruling deals with a confession of usurpation (ghaṣb) where the confessor later produces the item but claims it is defective or counterfeit. The Hanafi position is that the confessor's statement is accepted along with his oath, because he is admitting a liability but qualifying it. The burden of proof shifts to the claimant if he disputes the defect. This protects the confessor from being forced to pay for a sound item when he only admits to a defective one.

Key Terms
معيب (maʿīb): Defective, having a fault.
زيف (zuyūf): Counterfeit or of poor quality coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must pay for a sound cloth
B) His word is accepted with oath that it is the same cloth
C) He is not liable at all
D) The case is dismissed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a confessor who returns a defective item or counterfeit coins is believed on oath as to the defect, limiting his liability.

Confession of Numbers: Understanding Prepositions
Rulings on confession of amounts expressed with prepositions, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on confessions using 'from...to' and distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions, applying the principle that 'ila' excludes the endpoint.

Core Idea
'Ila' excludes the endpoint; the debtor owes the start through the number before the limit.

When a person confesses a debt using numbers and prepositions, the precise wording matters. The Hanafi school has a detailed approach to interpreting such phrases, especially 'from X to Y'. In this lesson, we examine two cases: multiplication with 'fi' and range with 'ila'. The latter reveals a subtle intra-Hanafi difference based on whether 'ila' includes or excludes the terminus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage covers two distinct scenarios: 'five in five' (multiplication) and 'from one dirham to ten' (range). Recognise that each involves a different preposition and interpretation.
The first uses 'fi' (in), the second uses 'ila' (to).
2
Parse the first case: 'five in five'
Determine the intended operation. If the debtor meant multiplication, only one 'five' is owed (i.e., 5). If he clarifies he meant addition ('five with five'), then ten is owed.
Default is multiplication unless explained otherwise.
3
Parse the second case: 'from one to ten'
Here, the key is the preposition 'ila' (to). Abu Hanifa holds that 'ila' excludes the endpoint, so the range is 1 through 9, i.e., nine dirhams. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that custom often includes the endpoint, so ten dirhams are owed.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the underlying principle
Abu Hanifa relies on the literal Arabic usage where 'ila' indicates a limit that is not included. The Sahibayn give weight to customary understanding, where 'from 1 to 10' commonly means including 10.
The principle is about the meaning of 'ghayah' (terminal point).
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's: nine dirhams. Later authorities (e.g., al-Hidayah) prefer this view.
This affects fatwa practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ خَمْسَةٌ فِي خَمْسَةٍ – يُرِيدُ الضَّرْبَ، وَالْحِسَابَ - لَزِمَهُ خَمْسَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ فَإِنْ قَالَ أَرَدْت خَمْسَةً مَعَ خَمْسَةٍ لَزِمَهُ عَشَرَةٌ وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ مِنْ دِرْهَم إِلَى عَشَرَةٍ لَزِمَهُ تِسْعَةٌ عِنْدَ أَبي حَنِيفَةَ يَلْزَمُهُ الِابْتِدَاءُ وَمَا بَعْدَهُ وَتَسْقُطُ الْغَايَةُ. وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ يَلْزَمُهُ الْعَشَرَةُ كُلُّهَا
And if he says, 'I owe you five in five' – meaning multiplication and calculation – then one five is binding. Then if he says, 'I intended five with five,' ten is binding. And if he says, 'I owe you from one dirham to ten,' according to Abu Hanifa nine is binding: the beginning and what follows are binding, and the end falls. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: all ten are binding.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage deals with confessions involving numbers with prepositions. When someone says 'five in five' intending multiplication (durub), only one five (i.e., five) is owed because it is interpreted as 'five multiplied by five' which is twenty-five? Actually the text says 'requires one five' which means just five? The Hanafi interpretation: 'five in five' can mean 'five times five' (25) but the confessor says he intended 'the multiplication and calculation' – that is ambiguous; the default is that only one unit of five is obligated. If he says 'I intended five with five', that is addition, so ten. The second part: 'from one dirham to ten' – Abu Hanifa says nine is binding because the range is inclusive of the beginning (one) and excludes the end (ten) based on the principle that 'ila' (to) excludes the terminal point in Arabic. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say all ten, interpreting 'ila' as inclusive. The settled fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الضرب (al-ḍarb): Multiplication in arithmetic.
الغاية (al-ghāyah): The terminal point or limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if someone confesses 'I owe you from one dirham to ten dirhams', how many dirhams does he owe?
A) Ten
B) Nine
C) Eleven
D) One

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the preposition 'ila' in confessions of range excludes the final number, so 'from one to ten' obligates nine dirhams, based on the literal meaning of the term 'ghayah'.

Confession with Quality Description: Continuity Matters
Intra-Hanafi dispute on credibility of confessor's qualification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on a confessor's qualification about coin quality and distinguish the ruling based on continuity (mawsūlan vs. mafsūlan).

Core Idea
A confessor's qualification is part of the confession only if stated continuously; otherwise it is a separate claim.

When someone confesses a debt but immediately adds that the coins are counterfeit, does the court believe the qualification? Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, differ on how to treat such statements. The key is whether the confession and qualification are said in one breath (mawsūlan) or with a pause (mafsūlan). This lesson unpacks the intra-Hanafi dispute and its practical effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal issue
The act here is an iqrar (confession) of a debt of 1000 dirhams as the price of goods, followed by the qualifier 'and they are counterfeit.' The issue is whether the confessor is believed about the quality.
The dispute is about the admissibility of a qualification that contradicts the apparent meaning of the confession.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that the confession of the debt is primary; the qualification is a contradictory claim against the claimant. Therefore, good coins (jiyād) are binding regardless of continuity.
His view ignores the timing of the qualification.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
The two disciples distinguish: if the confessor says everything in one continuous statement (mawsūlan), he is believed because the qualification is part of the same utterance. If he says it separately (mafsūlan), it is a new claim and he is not believed.
Continuity is the decisive factor for the Sahibayn.
4
Identify the settled (mufta bihi) view
Later Hanafi scholars adopt the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The mufta bihi ruling is: if mawsūlan, the confessor is believed that the coins are counterfeit; if mafsūlan, he must pay good coins.
This view balances the interests of both parties and is more nuanced.
5
Apply the ruling to a concrete case
Consider: Zayd says to Amr, 'I owe you 1000 dirhams from the price of cloth, and they are counterfeit' in one breath. Then Amr says 'They are good.' According to the mufta bihi, Zayd is believed and need only pay counterfeit coins. If Zayd said the same but paused between the confession and the qualification, he is not believed and must pay good coins.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is the quality of coins the confessor must pay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ أَلْفٌ مِنْ ثَمَنِ مَتَاعِ وَهِيَ زُيُوفٌ وَقَالَ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ هِيَ جِيَادٌ لَزِمَهُ الْحِيَادُ فِي قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ. وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ إِنْ قَالَ ذَلِكَ مَوْصُولًا صُدِّقَ ، وَإِنْ قَالَهُ مَفْصُولًا لَا يُصَدَّقُ
And if he says, 'I owe him one thousand from the price of goods, and they are counterfeit,' and the confesse says, 'They are good,' then good coins are binding according to Abu Hanifa. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: if he said that continuously (in one breath), he is believed; if he said it separately, he is not believed.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This ruling deals with a dispute about the quality of coins confessed. If the confessor adds immediately after the confession that the coins are counterfeit (zuyūf), Abu Hanifa holds that the good quality (jiyād) is binding regardless, because the confession of debt is primary and the qualification is a claim against the claimant. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad distinguish: if the confessor says the entire statement in one breath (mawsūlan), he is believed because the qualification is part of the confession; if he says it separately (mafsūlan), it is considered a new claim, and he is not believed. The settled position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, as it balances the interests.

Key Terms
موصولا (mawsūlan): In one continuous statement without pause.
مفصولا (mafsūlan): Separately, with a break between confession and qualification.
زُيُوفٌ (zuyūf): Counterfeit or defective coins.
جِيَادٌ (jiyād): Good, full-weight coins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone confesses 'I owe you 1000 dirhams' and then after a pause says 'they are counterfeit', according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?
A) He is believed
B) He is not believed
C) He must pay only half
D) The confession is void

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi ruling on a confessor's qualification of coin quality depends on whether it was stated continuously (mawsūlan) or separately (mafsūlan).

Confession of Composite Objects: Ring, Sword, Curtain
Ruling on included parts in confession of composite objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the integral parts included when confessing to a ring, sword, or curtain according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Confessing to an object includes its customary integral parts unless restricted.

When someone acknowledges ownership of a composite item in favor of another, the Hanafi school clarifies which components are automatically included. This passage from Al-Quduri lists the parts that form the complete object. Understanding these defaults helps in cases where the confession is general and no exceptions are made.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objects
The passage covers three composite objects: ring (khātam), sword (sayf), and curtain or canopy (ḥajalah).
These are examples; the principle applies to other composite objects by analogy.
2
List the included parts for each
For a ring: the metal setting (khalqah) and gemstone (faṣṣ). For a sword: the blade (naṣl), handle (ḥifn), and shoulder strap (ḥamāʾil). For a canopy: the wooden rods (ʿīdān) and cloth covering (kiswah).
Memorize these specific pairings as they are defaults.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The confession applies to the entire customary object. The parts listed are those customarily considered integral. The confessor may restrict by specifically excluding a part.
The default is inclusion; exclusion requires explicit statement.
4
Apply to new cases
When encountering a new composite object, ask: what parts are customarily considered integral? For example, a car confession includes the engine and wheels, not floor mats.
Custom (ʿurf) determines integration, not physical attachment alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ لِغَيْرِهِ بِخَاتَم فَلَهُ الخَلْقَةُ، وَالْفَصُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ لَهُ بِسَيْفِ فَلَهُ النَّصْلُ، وَالْحِفْنُ ، وَالْحَمَائِلُ وَمَنْ أَقَرَّ بِحَجَلَةٍ فَلَهُ الْعِيدَانُ، وَالْكِسْوَةُ
And whoever confesses to another of a ring, then the setting and the stone belong to him. And whoever confesses to him of a sword, then the blade, the handle, and the strap belong to him. And whoever confesses of a curtain, then the rods and the covering belong to him.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage clarifies that when a person confesses ownership of a composite item in favor of another, all integral parts are included unless otherwise specified. For a ring (khātam), the 'khalqah' (the metal setting) and 'fass' (the gemstone) are included. For a sword (sayf), the 'nasl' (blade), 'hifn' (hilt/handle), and 'hamā'il' (belt/strap) are included. For a 'hajalah' (a curtained canopy), the 'ʿīdān' (wooden rods) and 'kiswah' (covering cloth) are included. The Hanafi principle is that the confession of an object includes its components that are customarily considered part of it, unless the confessor restricts it.

Key Terms
الخاتم (al-khātam): A signet ring.
الخلقة (al-khalqah): The metal band or setting of the ring.
الفص (al-faṣṣ): The gemstone set in the ring.
النصل (al-naṣl): The blade of the sword.
الحفن (al-ḥifn): The handle or hilt of the sword.
الحمائل (al-ḥamāʾil): The shoulder strap or belt for carrying the sword.
الحجلة (al-ḥajalah): A bridal canopy or curtained structure.
العيدان (al-ʿīdān): The wooden rods or poles of the canopy.
الكسوة (al-kiswah): The cloth covering of the canopy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone confesses 'I owe you a sword', what parts are included according to the text?
A) Only the blade
B) Blade and handle
C) Blade, handle, and strap
D) Only the handle

Key Takeaway
When confessing a composite object, all customary integral parts are included unless excluded.

Ruling on Numerical Statements in Acknowledgments: The Dispute Over 'From...to...'
States three Hanafi positions on ambiguous numerical expressions in acknowledgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether the endpoint is included in a numerical range in acknowledgments and explain the basis for the disagreement.

Core Idea
In Hanafi iqrar, 'from X to Y' excludes the endpoint unless context demands otherwise — but the Sahibayn disagree.

When someone acknowledges a debt with a range like 'from one to ten,' does that include ten? The answer depends on which Hanafi imam you follow. This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi dispute on this issue, a classic example of how linguistic interpretation affects the amount owed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The text presents two scenarios: a clear expression ('five with five') and an ambiguous one ('from one to ten'). Recognize that the first has no disagreement.
2
State the agreed outcome for the clear case
All imams agree that 'five with five' means ten dirhams are owed. This is binding.
3
State Abu Hanifa's ruling for the ambiguous case
Abu Hanifa holds that 'from one to ten' binds nine dirhams because the particle 'to' ('ila') excludes the endpoint. The beginning and what follows are included, but the ghayah (end) is dropped.
4
State the Sahibayn's ruling
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that all ten dirhams are binding because 'from...to' is commonly understood to include both ends.
5
Analyze the linguistic basis
The disagreement stems from the interpretation of 'ila' (to/up to). Abu Hanifa treats it as exclusive; the Sahibayn treat it as inclusive. This reflects different usul on the meaning of particles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قَالَ أَرَدْت خَمْسَةَ مَعَ خَمْسَةٍ لَزِمَهُ عَشَرَةٌ وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ مِنْ دِرْهَم إِلَى عَشَرَةٍ لَزِمَهُ تِسْعَةٌ عِنْدَ أَبي حَنِيفَةَ يَلْزَمُهُ الِابْتِدَاءُ وَمَا بَعْدَهُ وَتَسْقُطُ الْغَايَةُ. وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٌ يَلْزَمُهُ الْعَشَرَةُ كُلُّهَا
If he said: I intended five with five, then ten are binding. And if he said: for him upon me from one dirham to ten, then nine are binding according to Abu Hanifa: the beginning and what follows it are binding, and the end is dropped. And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: all ten are binding.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the interpretation of numerical expressions in acknowledgments. The passage presents two cases: (1) 'I intended five with five' clearly means ten, so all agree. (2) 'from one dirham to ten' is ambiguous: does it include the final number ten or not? Abu Hanifa interprets 'ila' (to) as exclusive of the end, so nine are owed. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that such phrasing includes both ends, so ten are owed. The text does not specify which view is preferred.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right or debt, making it binding.
غاية (ghayah): The endpoint or limit mentioned in a numerical range, e.g., 'ten' in 'from one to ten'.
ابتداء (ibtida'): The starting point of a range, which is always included.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'Upon me from one dirham to ten', how many dirhams are binding according to Abu Hanifa?
A) One
B) Nine
C) Ten
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi iqrar, the interpretation of 'from...to...' is disputed: Abu Hanifa excludes the endpoint (nine), while the Sahibayn include it (ten); the preferred view often follows Abu Hanifa.

Rulings on Acknowledgment of Debt from Unreceived Sales and Prohibited Goods
Ruling statements on acknowledgment linked to sale price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and distinguish the Hanafi ruling on acknowledgment of a debt from the price of a slave not received (specified vs unspecified) and from the price of prohibited items.

Core Idea
An acknowledgment of debt is binding even if based on an illegal transaction, but if tied to a specific unreceived sale, the creditor may choose to complete the exchange.

This passage from Al-Quduri's matn addresses two distinct scenarios of acknowledgment (iqrar) where the debt is claimed to arise from the price of an item. The first involves a slave not yet taken possession of; the second involves prohibited goods like wine or swine. Understanding these rulings clarifies how Hanafi law treats acknowledgments that reference underlying transactions, separating the validity of the debt from the legality of the sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
Recognize that the passage covers: (a) acknowledgment for the price of a slave not received, and (b) acknowledgment for the price of wine or swine.
Both scenarios involve a debt that is acknowledged (iqrar) but the underlying sale is problematic.
2
Analyze the slave scenario: specified vs unspecified
If the acknowledger names a specific slave, the creditor may either deliver that slave and take the thousand, or forfeit the claim. If the acknowledger does not specify the slave but says the price is for a slave not received, the thousand is binding according to Abu Hanifa.
The specification affects the creditor's option.
3
Understand the ruling for prohibited goods
If the acknowledgment is for the price of wine or swine, the debt is binding and the acknowledger's explanation (that the transaction was illegal) is not accepted.
The debt stands regardless of the illegal nature of the underlying contract.
4
Apply the legal reasoning
The acknowledgment itself creates a liability independent of the underlying transaction. The law does not allow the debtor to escape liability by invoking the illegality of the source.
This is based on the principle that iqrar is binding as a declaration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ أَلْفَ مِنْ ثَمَن عَبْدِ اشْتَرَيْته مِنْهُ وَلَم أَقْبِضْهُ فَإِنْ ذَكَرَ عَبْدًا بِعَيْنِهِ قِيلَ لِلْمُقَرِ لَهُ إِنْ شِنْت فَسَلَّمُ الْعَبْدَ وَخُذْ الْأَلْفَ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا شَيْءَ لَكَ وَإِنْ قَالَ مِنْ ثَمَنِ عَبْدِ لَمْ يُعَيّنْهُ إِلَّا أَي لَمَّ أَقْبِضْهُ لَزِمَهُ الْأَلْفُ فِي قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ أَلْفُ دِرْهَم مِنْ ثَمَن خَمْرٍ، أَوْ خِنْزِيرِ لَزِمَهُ الْأَلْفُ وَلَمْ يُقْبَلْ تَفْسِيرُهُ
And if he said: for him upon me a thousand from the price of a slave I bought from him and I did not take possession, then if he specified a particular slave, it is said to the acknowledged party: if you wish, deliver the slave and take the thousand, otherwise you have nothing. And if he said from the price of a slave without specifying, except that he said 'I did not take possession,' the thousand is binding according to Abu Hanifa. And if he said: for him upon me a thousand dirhams from the price of wine or swine, the thousand is binding and his explanation is not accepted.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage covers two scenarios of acknowledgment tied to a price. First, if the acknowledger says he owes a thousand from the price of a slave he bought but did not take possession, and he names the slave, the creditor may either deliver the slave and get the thousand, or forfeit the claim. Second, if he does not name the slave but says 'I did not take possession,' then Abu Hanifa says the thousand is binding (the condition is considered unfulfilled). Third, if the acknowledgment is for the price of wine or swine (prohibited), the acknowledged amount is binding and his attempt to explain it away is not accepted, because the underlying transaction is void but the acknowledgment itself creates a debt.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): Price or payment for a sale.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession of a purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone acknowledges a debt of 1000 dirhams as the price of wine, what is the ruling?
A) The acknowledgment is void because wine is haram
B) He must pay 1000 dirhams and his explanation is not accepted
C) He can pay half
D) He must pay but can deduct the value of the wine

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an acknowledgment of debt is binding and independent of the legality or completion of the underlying transaction, subject to specific conditions like specification of the item.
